<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Ecology &amp; Evolution 2017 1:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-02-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Carnivorous plants exploit animals as a nutritional source and have inspired long-standing questions about the origin and evolution of carnivory-related traits. To investigate the molecular bases of carnivory, we sequenced the genome of the heterophyllous pitcher plant Cephalotus follicularis, in which we succeeded in regulating the developmental switch between carnivorous and non-carnivorous leaves. Transcriptome comparison of the two leaf types and gene repertoire analysis identified genetic changes associated with prey attraction, capture, digestion and nutrient absorption. Analysis of digestive fluid proteins from C. follicularis and three other carnivorous plants with independent carnivorous origins revealed repeated co-options of stress-responsive protein lineages coupled with convergent amino acid substitutions to acquire digestive physiology. These results imply constraints on the available routes to evolve plant carnivory. The Australian pitcher plant (Cephalotus sp.) genome reveals genetic changes associated with prey attraction, capture, digestion and nutrient absorption. Comparison with other carnivorous plants reveals constraints on evolution of plant carnivory"/>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Carnivorous plants exploit animals as a nutritional source and have inspired long-standing questions about the origin and evolution of carnivory-related traits. To investigate the molecular bases of carnivory, we sequenced the genome of the heterophyllous pitcher plant <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i>, in which we succeeded in regulating the developmental switch between carnivorous and non-carnivorous leaves. Transcriptome comparison of the two leaf types and gene repertoire analysis identified genetic changes associated with prey attraction, capture, digestion and nutrient absorption. Analysis of digestive fluid proteins from <i>C. follicularis</i> and three other carnivorous plants with independent carnivorous origins revealed repeated co-options of stress-responsive protein lineages coupled with convergent amino acid substitutions to acquire digestive physiology. These results imply constraints on the available routes to evolve plant carnivory.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/s41559-016-0059.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Carnivorous plants bear extensively modified leaves capable of attracting, trapping and digesting small animals, and absorbing the released nutrients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1155">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Darwin, C. Insectivorous Plants (D. Appleton and Company, 1875)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1158">2</a></sup>. Plant carnivory evolved independently in several lineages of flowering plants, providing a classic model for the study of convergent evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Albert, V. A., Williams, S. E. &amp; Chase, M. W. Carnivorous plants: phylogeny and structural evolution. Science 257, 1491–1495 (1992)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1162">3</a></sup>. <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> (<i>Cephalotus</i>), a carnivorous plant native to southwest Australia that belongs to the monospecific family Cephalotaceae in the order Oxalidales, forms both carnivorous pitcher leaves and non-carnivorous flat leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Co-existence of the two types of leaf in a single individual plant provides a unique opportunity to understand the genetic basis of plant carnivory through comparative analysis of these serially homologous organs. To this end, we sequenced the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome. A total of 305 Gb of Illumina reads were generated for contig assembly and scaffolding, and 17 Gb of PacBio reads for inter-contig gap filling (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The resulting assembly consists of 16,307 scaffolds totalling 1.61 Gb with an N50 length of 287 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 2</a>), corresponding to 76% of the estimated genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1a</a>). Long-terminal repeat retrotransposons account for 76% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 3 and 4</a>). Syntenic block comparison with the robusta coffee genome, which maintained diploidy since the ancient split from the <i>Cephalotus</i> lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1195">4</a></sup>, reveals mostly one-to-one mappings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 5</a>), indicating that the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome has not experienced further whole genome duplications since the hexaploidy event at the origin of core eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1208">5</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Note 1</a>). We annotated 36,503 protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1b–e</a>), and 72 microRNA (miRNA) loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 6</a>) and their potential targets (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 7</a>) using RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data of representative tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 8–10</a>). Orthologous gene groups (orthogroups) were defined using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1228">6</a></sup> for the complete gene sets of <i>Cephalotus</i> and eight eudicot species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 11 and 12</a>). Analysis of shared singletons indicates that core eudicot genes are conserved in the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Note 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 13</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Cephalotus morphology and genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>Cephalotus</i> morphology and genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="267"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Pitcher and flat leaves. <b>b</b>, Flat and pitcher leaves predominantly produced at 15 °C and 25 °C, respectively, under continuous light conditions. Diamonds and error bars indicate means and standard deviations, respectively. Each filled circle represents an independent experiment with 45 plants. <b>c</b>, Synteny block matching of the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome against the coffee genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1274">4</a></sup> revealed a one-to-one matching in most genomic loci.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Maximum-likelihood gene gain and loss analysis detected lineage-specific expansion of 492 orthogroups in <i>Cephalotus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Gene ontology (GO) enrichment analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 15–21</a>) highlighted <i>Cephalotus</i>-expanded orthogroups containing purple acid phosphatases, known as a typical component of digestive fluids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1301">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Gallie, D. R. &amp; Chang, S. C. Signal transduction in the carnivorous plant Sarracenia purpurea. Regulation of secretory hydrolase expression during development and in response to resources. Plant Physiol. 115, 1461–1471 (1997)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1304">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 17</a>). RNase T2, also known as a constituent of digestive fluids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1312">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Okabe, T., Yoshimoto, I., Hitoshi, M., Ogawa, T. &amp; Ohyama, T. An S-like ribonuclease gene is used to generate a trap-leaf enzyme in the carnivorous plant Drosera adelae . FEBS Lett. 579, 5729–5733 (2005)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1315">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nishimura, E. et al. S-like ribonuclease gene expression in carnivorous plants. Planta 238, 955–967 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1318">9</a></sup>, is enriched among orthogroups composed only of genes from <i>Cephalotus</i> and another carnivorous plant <i>Utricularia gibba</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 18</a>). Also, the enriched GO term ‘cellular response to nitrogen levels’ included ten <i>Cephalotus</i>-specific singleton genes encoding dihydropyrimidinases, which have the potential function of acquired nitrogen recycling (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Nitrogen is, in turn, known to be one of the primary limiting nutrients that carnivorous plants derive from prey<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1338">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ellison, A. M. Nutrient limitation and stoichiometry of carnivorous plants. Plant Biol. 8, 740–747 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1341">10</a></sup>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>As we succeeded in regulating the developmental switch between pitcher and flat leaves by ambient temperature (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1f,g</a>), their transcriptomes were compared. The pitcher transcriptome was differentially enriched with cell cycle- and morphogenesis-related GO terms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 22</a>), which may reflect the morphological complexity of pitcher leaves. Although both developmental and thermoresponsive genes may change their expression in the temperature-dependent leaf switching, certain developmental regulators related to adaxial–abaxial polarity (for example, <i>AS2</i>, <i>YAB5</i>, and <i>WOX1</i> orthologues) showed higher expression levels in shoot apices bearing pitchers than those terminating in flat leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), implying the involvement of such factors in pitcher development and evolution. In contrast, the flat leaf transcriptome was enriched with photosynthesis-related GO terms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 23</a>). These results are compatible with the distinct functional specializations of carnivory-dominated pitcher leaves versus photosynthesis-dominated flat leaves.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Carnivorous plants attract potential prey by nectar, coloration and scent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1376">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Król, E. et al. Quite a few reasons for calling carnivores ‘the most wonderful plants in the world’. Ann. Bot. 109, 47–64 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1379">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Adlassnig, W., Peroutka, M. &amp; Lendl, T. Traps of carnivorous pitcher plants as a habitat: composition of the fluid, biodiversity and mutualistic activities. Ann. Bot. 107, 181–194 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1382">12</a></sup>. GO terms enriched in the pitcher transcriptome included ‘starch metabolic process’ and ‘sucrose metabolic process’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 22</a>), which may be related to the production of attractive nectar. Indeed, we detected transcriptional upregulation of certain sucrose biosynthetic genes and members of sugar efflux carriers in pitcher leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The epidermis of carnivorous pitfall traps often develops a slippery, waxy surface that promotes prey capture and prevents them from escaping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1395">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Whitney, H. M. &amp; Federle, W. Biomechanics of plant–insect interactions. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 105–111 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1398">13</a></sup>. A cytochrome P450 (<i>CYP</i>) orthogroup was expanded in the <i>Cephalotus</i> lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 14</a>). In a phylo­genetic tree, these <i>CYP</i> genes belonged to a clade containing <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes involved in wax and cutin biosynthesis (<i>CYP86</i> and <i>CYP96A</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bak, S. et al. Cytochromes P450. Arab. B. 9, e0144 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1424">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). These genes, as well as wax ester synthase orthologues (<i>WSD1</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, F. et al. Identification of the wax ester synthase/acyl-coenzyme A: diacylglycerol acyltransferase WSD1 required for stem wax ester biosynthesis in Arabidopsis . Plant Physiol. 148, 97–107 (2008)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1435">15</a></sup>, showed pitcher-predominant expression and are tandemly duplicated in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), suggesting possible co-regulated involvement of the clusters in slippery surface formation.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Carnivorous plants secrete digestive enzymes for degradation of trapped animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1446">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Król, E. et al. Quite a few reasons for calling carnivores ‘the most wonderful plants in the world’. Ann. Bot. 109, 47–64 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1449">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Adlassnig, W., Peroutka, M. &amp; Lendl, T. Traps of carnivorous pitcher plants as a habitat: composition of the fluid, biodiversity and mutualistic activities. Ann. Bot. 107, 181–194 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1452">12</a></sup>. Previous studies on several digestive enzymes of <i>Nepenthes</i> spp., <i>Drosera</i> spp., <i>Dionaea muscipula</i> and <i>Cephalotus</i> indicate that pathogenesis-related proteins were co-opted for digestive function as well as for preventing microbial colonization of digestive fluid (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Buch, F. et al. Secreted pitfall-trap fluid of carnivorous Nepenthes plants is unsuitable for microbial growth. Ann. Bot. 111, 375–383 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1468">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bemm, F. et al. Venus flytrap carnivorous lifestyle builds on herbivore defense strategies. Genome Res. 26, 1–14 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1471">19</a> and refs in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>). To further investigate the origin and evolution of digestive enzymes of <i>Cephalotus</i> and three other distantly related carnivorous plants (<i>Drosera adelae</i>, <i>N. alata</i> and <i>Sarracenia purpurea</i>), we sequenced fragments of digestive fluid proteins and identified 35 corresponding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 25–28</a>). As <i>Drosera</i> and <i>Nepenthes</i> trace back to a common carnivorous origin in Caryophyllales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Albert, V. A., Williams, S. E. &amp; Chase, M. W. Carnivorous plants: phylogeny and structural evolution. Science 257, 1491–1495 (1992)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1503">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Heubl, G., Bringmann, G. &amp; Meimberg, H. Molecular phylogeny and character evolution of carnivorous plant families in Caryophyllales — revisited. Plant Biol. 8, 821–830 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1506">20</a></sup>, the four species including <i>Cephalotus</i> therefore cover three independent origins of plant carnivory. Together with previously identified enzyme sequences including proteins from <i>Dionaea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>), we inferred phylo­genetic relationships among the digestive fluid proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5a–ah</a>). Glycoside hydrolase family 19 (GH19) chitinase, β-1,3-glucanase, PR-1-like protein, thaumatin-like protein, purple acid phosphatase and RNase T2 genes showed orthologous relationships among carnivores despite their multiple origins. This result suggests that orthologous genes were repeatedly co-opted for digestive functions in independent carnivorous plant lineages.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Orthologue–paralogue relationships and carnivory-related expression of digestive fluid proteins."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Orthologue–paralogue relationships and carnivory-related expression of digestive fluid proteins.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="756"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic relationships of independently evolved carnivorous plants and, to their right, the digestive fluid proteins identified through proteomic analysis. Polytomy in the tree represents topological discrepancy between previously reported plastid and nuclear phylogenies (see Methods). Brackets connect protein variants likely to originate from the same gene. <b>b</b>, Phylogeny-based orthologue–paralogue classification. Branch colours denote species identities. Magenta on internal nodes indicates inferred duplication events. Gene numbers in collapsed clades are shown next to triangles. The collapsed clades do not contain genes encoding the digestive fluid proteins but may contain other <i>Cephalotus</i> genes as well as non-carnivorous plant genes. Complete trees are available in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, Transcriptome comparison of flat and pitcher organs in <i>Cephalotus</i> (<b>c</b>) and <i>N. alata</i> (<b>d</b>). Red numbers indicate positions of genes encoding digestive fluid proteins identified in this work (1–21, shown in <b>a</b>) and previous studies (22–25, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>), several of which are outliers showing trap-specific expression.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>To infer putative ancestral functions of these independently arisen digestive fluid proteins, we examined the expression patterns of their phylogenetically most closely related <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5a–ah</a>). Compared with other genes in the same families, these <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes showed a significant tendency to be upregulated on various biotic and abiotic stresses (<i>P </i>&lt; 0.02, randomization test) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5ai</a>). This result suggests that co-option from stress-responsive proteins is a general evolutionary trend in the repeated evolution of carnivorous plant enzymes. Whether they are currently bifunctional—having both carnivorous and non-carnivorous roles—is unclear, but tissue-specific basal expression is probably optimized for carnivory in <i>Cephalotus</i> and <i>N. alata</i>, as the genes are preferentially expressed in their pitcher traps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Fig. 2c,d</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>In <i>Cephalotus</i>, three aspartic proteases were identified in the digestive fluid proteome. We found three genomic clusters of aspartic protease genes containing both pitcher-preferential and constitutively expressed genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5a–c</a>). Together with the inferred tandem duplications of <i>CYP</i>, this result highlights the roles of gene duplication and subsequent functional divergence in carnivorous plant evolution.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The repeated evolutionary utilization of similar genes may have been accompanied by convergent responses to carnivory-specific selective pressures at the amino acid substitution level. To test this, we developed a tree-based method for the detection of molecular convergence in multigene families, using phylogeny reconciliation between third codon position-derived gene trees and a consensus species tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Note 3</a>, see Methods for the choice of a species tree). Using reconciled trees, the number of digestive enzyme-specific convergent substitutions was inferred on the basis of Bayesian ancestral sequence reconstructions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). By comparing convergent substitution numbers and empirically calculated background-level expectations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Castoe, T. A. et al. Evidence for an ancient adaptive episode of convergent molecular evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 8986–8991 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1634">21</a></sup>, we found that GH19 chitinases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a>), purple acid phosphatases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 6i,j</a>) and RNase T2s (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 6m,n</a>) significantly accumulated convergent amino acid substitutions. For all three enzymes, two pitfall-type carnivorous pitcher plants, <i>Cephalotus</i> and <i>N. alata</i>, were associated as convergent branch pairs. Furthermore, for RNase T2, significant molecular convergence was also detected between <i>Cephalotus</i> and the common ancestor of the three Caryophyllales species, <i>D. adelae</i>, <i>D. muscipula</i> and <i>N. alata</i>, which produce sticky, snap and pitfall traps, respectively. Parsimonious inference of character evolution indicates that trapping strategy diversified after the establishment of carnivory in the Caryophyllales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Albert, V. A., Williams, S. E. &amp; Chase, M. W. Carnivorous plants: phylogeny and structural evolution. Science 257, 1491–1495 (1992)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1667">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Heubl, G., Bringmann, G. &amp; Meimberg, H. Molecular phylogeny and character evolution of carnivorous plant families in Caryophyllales — revisited. Plant Biol. 8, 821–830 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1670">20</a></sup>. Therefore, molecular adaptation of RNase T2 probably occurred both during the evolution of carnivory and subsequently during the establishment of the specific capture strategy of pitfall traps. It is noteworthy that the <i>Cephalotus</i> RNase T2 and purple acid phosphatase genes are located adjacent to each other within a 40 kb interval of the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). This placement could indicate an arrangement favoured by adaptive, positionally correlated co-expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Michalak, P. Coexpression, coregulation, and cofunctionality of neighboring genes in eukaryotic genomes. Genomics 91, 243–248 (2008)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1684">22</a></sup> of these modified carnivorous enzymes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Note 4</a>). In light of similar cases of convergent evolution shown for animal digestive enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Stewart, C. B., Schilling, J. W. &amp; Wilson, A. C. Adaptive evolution in the stomach lysozymes of foregut fermenters. Nature 330, 401–404 (1987)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1691">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zhang, J. Parallel adaptive origins of digestive RNases in Asian and African leaf monkeys. Nat. Genet. 38, 819–823 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1694">24</a></sup>, we propose that major changes in nutritional strategy impose a selective pressure strong enough to override evolutionary contingency in both plants and animals.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Molecular convergence of digestive enzymes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Molecular convergence of digestive enzymes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-016-0059/MediaObjects/41559_2016_Article_BFs415590160059_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="219"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, GH19 chitinase phylogeny obtained from the phylogeny reconciliation. Identified digestive enzyme genes are indicated by trap illustrations. Magenta on internal nodes indicates inferred duplication events. The bar indicates 0.1 nucleotide substitutions per site. The complete tree is available in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 6q</a>. <b>b</b>, Accumulation of convergent amino acid substitutions in GH19 chitinases. The positions of digestive enzyme branch pairs are indicated by red circles with corresponding numbers in <b>a</b>. Grey tones indicate branch pair density. The line shows a linear regression. <b>c</b>, Relationships between substitution processes and amino acid exposure in protein structures. As the convergent branch pairs in different families showed similar patterns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 8e</a>), data from GH19 chitinases, purple acid phosphatases and RNase T2s are pooled. The bottom panel shows posterior probabilities (PP) of convergent (pink) and divergent (light blue) substitution pairs. The top panel shows density distributions of convergent and divergent loci (PP ≥ 0.5, filled pink and light blue in the lower panel) as well as non-detected positions (PP &lt; 0.5, filled grey with outline colour according to the substitution types). <i>P</i> value indicates a statistical difference of medians (vertical lines) (randomization test).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>As protein structure imposes major constraints on amino acid substitutions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Choi, S. S., Vallender, E. J. &amp; Lahn, B. T. Systematically assessing the influence of 3-dimensional structural context on the molecular evolution of mammalian proteomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 2131–2133 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1742">25</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Goldman, N., Thorne, J. L. &amp; Jones, D. T. Assessing the impact of secondary structure and solvent accessibility on protein evolution. Genetics 149, 445–458 (1998)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1745">27</a></sup>, we mapped amino acid residues identified as convergent onto corresponding 3D enzyme models. Convergent positions do not overlap with or cluster around catalytically essential amino acids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Instead, they tend to be located at exposed positions to an extent comparable to divergent substitutions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>), despite the prediction that more exposed positions result in lower convergence probability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Goldstein, R. A., Pollard, S. T., Shah, S. D. &amp; Pollock, D. D. Nonadaptive amino acid convergence rates decrease over time. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1373–1381 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1755">28</a></sup>. Exposed sites are structurally less constrained, and substitutions in such sites are likely to change their interactions with other molecules in solution, rather than changing protein conformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Choi, S. S., Vallender, E. J. &amp; Lahn, B. T. Systematically assessing the influence of 3-dimensional structural context on the molecular evolution of mammalian proteomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 2131–2133 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1759">25</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Goldman, N., Thorne, J. L. &amp; Jones, D. T. Assessing the impact of secondary structure and solvent accessibility on protein evolution. Genetics 149, 445–458 (1998)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1762">27</a></sup>. During the evolution of digestive enzymes, selective pressures may have come from the digestive fluid environment, which include the presence of insect-derived substrates, high endogenous proteolytic activity, low pH and microbial invasion or symbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1767">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Król, E. et al. Quite a few reasons for calling carnivores ‘the most wonderful plants in the world’. Ann. Bot. 109, 47–64 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1770">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Adlassnig, W., Peroutka, M. &amp; Lendl, T. Traps of carnivorous pitcher plants as a habitat: composition of the fluid, biodiversity and mutualistic activities. Ann. Bot. 107, 181–194 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1773">12</a></sup>. As exposed residues constitute the protein–environment interface, the convergent amino acid substitutions may have been critical factors for the convergent establishment of carnivory across the angiosperms.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>In the final phase of carnivorous plant physiology, digested molecules are absorbed into the plant body to promote growth and reproduction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Juniper, B. E., Robins, R. J. &amp; Joel, D. M. The Carnivorous Plants (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1780">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ellison, A. M. &amp; Gotelli, N. J. Energetics and the evolution of carnivorous plants—Darwin’s ‘most wonderful plants in the world’. J. Exp. Bot. 60, 19–42 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1783">29</a></sup>. We found that various transporters were preferentially expressed in pitcher leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 29</a>). One pitcher-predominant transporter showed phylogenetic affinity to the <i>AMMONIUM TRANSPORTER 1</i> (<i>AMT1</i>) subfamily (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), which contains the previously characterized carnivory-related <i>D. muscipula</i> gene <i>DmAMT1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Scherzer, S. et al. The Dionaea muscipula ammonium channel DmAMT1 provides NH4 + uptake associated with Venus flytrap’s prey digestion. Curr. Biol. 23, 1649–1657 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1805">30</a></sup>. This result, together with the repeated co-option of digestive enzymes already described, indicates utilization of common genetic programs and evolutionary pathways in independently evolved carnivorous plant lineages.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The <i>Cephalotus</i> genome has allowed us to discover numerous genes associated with evolutionary transition to carnivory in plants. In particular, the high degree of convergent evolution in digestive enzymes indicates that there are few available evolutionary pathways for angiosperms to become carnivorous.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Plant materials and culture conditions</h3><p>Axenically grown plants of <i>C. follicularis</i> were obtained from CZ Plants Nursery (Trebovice, Czech Republic) and were maintained in polycarbonate containers (60 × 60 × 100 mm) containing half-strength Murashige and Skoog solid medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Murashige, T. &amp; Skoog, F. A revised medium for rapid growth and bio assays with tobacco tissue cultures. Physiol. Plant. 15, 473–497 (1962)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1830">31</a></sup> supplemented with 3% sucrose, 1× Gamborg’s vitamins, 0.1% 2-(N-morpholino)ethanesulfonic acid, 0.05% Plant Preservative Mixture (Plant Cell Technology) and 0.3% Phytagel, at 25 °C in continuous light. For transcriptome sequencings, <i>D. adelae</i> was cultivated in a peat pot in an incubator at 25 °C in continuous light. <i>N. alata</i> was grown in soil in a greenhouse. <i>S. purpurea</i> was grown in peat-based soil and was maintained in a field. For digestive fluid sampling, <i>C. follicularis</i>, <i>D. adelae</i>, <i>N. alata</i> and <i>S. purpurea</i> were grown in a greenhouse.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Culture conditions for leaf fate regulation</h3><p>Shoot apices with one or two expanded leaves were collected with fine forceps from plants grown at 25 °C and planted on medium. The plantlets were grown for 12 weeks under a light intensity of 20–40 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. Numbers of youngest pitcher and flat leaves on main shoots were counted for each plantlet (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Leaves with intermediate shapes were counted as either of the two categories based on morphological similarity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">DNA isolation</h3><p>Total genomic DNA was isolated from young flat leaves and pitcher leaves of axenically grown plants. Collected leaves were homogenized in liquid nitrogen using a mortar and pestle. The homogenate was transferred into 2× CTAB extraction buffer (2% cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB), 1.4 M NaCl, 100 mM Tris-HCl (pH 8.0), 20 mM EDTA (pH 8.0)) preheated to 80 °C and was gently agitated at 60 °C for 1 h. An equal volume of chloroform:isoamyl alcohol (25:1) was added and agitated using a rotator at 20 r.p.m. for 10 min at room temperature. After centrifugation at 9,000 × <i>g</i> for 30 min at room temperature, supernatants were transferred to new tubes and supplemented with 1/10 volume of 10% CTAB and an equal volume of chloroform:isoamyl alcohol (25:1). The tubes were shaken with a rotator for 10 min. After centrifugation, supernatants were again transferred to new tubes and an equal volume of isopropanol was added. The tubes were centrifuged and supernatants were discarded. The crude DNA pellet was rinsed with 5 ml of 70% EtOH and air-dried for 10 min. The pellet was dissolved in 200 μl of TE (pH 8.0) containing 0.1 mg/ml RNase A, and gently agitated for 60 min at 37 °C. A 1/20 volume of 20 mg ml<sup>−1</sup> Proteinase K was added, and tubes were incubated at 56 °C for 30 min. Subsequently, the DNA solution was further purified using Qiagen Genomic-tip, following the manufacturer’s instructions. DNA concentration was determined using fluorometry with Qubit 2.0 (Life Technologies).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Whole-genome shotgun short-read sequences were generated with an Illumina HiSeq 2000 to a depth of approximately 150-fold of the 2 Gb <i>Cephalotus</i> genome using paired-end and mate-pair protocols, according to the manufacturer’s instructions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 1</a>). For long read sequencing, genomic DNA samples were sheared to 6 kb or 10 kb using g-Tube (Covaris, Massachusetts). Libraries were prepared with DNA Template Prep Kit 2.0 (Pacific Biosciences, California) (3–10 kb) following the manufacturer’s instructions and sequencing was performed using PacBio RS with C2 chemistry, P2 polymerase and 45-min movies. Using 158 cells, a total of ca. 17 Gb were generated with a quality cut-off value of 0.75 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome size estimation</h3><p>The size of the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome was estimated by k-mer frequency analysis using JELLYFISH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1913">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome assembly</h3><p>Illumina paired-end reads with all insert sizes, and mate-pair reads with insert sizes of 2 and 5 kb, were first assembled into 43,308 scaffolds using Allpaths-LG v42381<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1928">33</a></sup>. Fragment filling was applied to paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 170 bp and 250 bp. Standard deviations of insert sizes were set to 10% of insert sizes. Gap filling and further scaffolding were performed by adding mate-pair reads with longer inserts using SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V, Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1932">34</a></sup> and GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1936">35</a></sup>. PacBio reads were subjected to two rounds of error correction using Sprai v0.2.2.3 (<a href="http://zombie.cb.k.u-tokyo.ac.jp/sprai/">http://zombie.cb.k.u-tokyo.ac.jp/sprai/</a>) and used for four rounds of iterative gap filling with PBJelly v12.9.14<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1947">36</a></sup>. The final assembly included 16,307 scaffolds with N50 of 287 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Repeat identification</h3><p>Repetitive elements of the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome were first identified and masked for gene prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 3 and 4</a>). <i>De novo</i> prediction of transposable elements was performed using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) and LTR_FINDER (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/</a>). Known transposable elements were found using RepeatMasker and RepeatProteinMask (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org">http://repeatmasker.org</a>). Tandem repeat sequences were screened using Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d41612e1994">37</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">RNA extraction</h3><p>Plant materials were ground in liquid nitrogen using a mortar and pestle. Total RNA was extracted using the PureLink Plant RNA Reagent (Life Technologies) and subsequently purified using the RNeasy Mini Kit (QIAGEN). DNase treatment was performed during the column purification. Total RNA was qualified using a 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Extracted RNA was subjected to two rounds of mRNA enrichment using Dynabeads mRNA Purification Kit (Life Technologies) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. RNA-seq libraries were prepared using TruSeq RNA Sample Preparation kit v.2 (Illumina). Strand-specific mRNA libraries were constructed using the dUTP second-strand marking method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Levin, J. Z. et al. Comprehensive comparative analysis of strand-specific RNA sequencing methods. Nat. Methods 7, 709–715 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2014">38</a></sup>. These libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 with three biological replications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Gene prediction</h3><p>For gene predictions, we used homology-based, <i>ab inito</i> and transcript-based methods. Protein data sets of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>, <i>Manihot esculenta</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Ricinus communis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 11</a>) were aligned to the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome using tblastn (cut-off: 1e−5) and then homology-based gene predictions were generated using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547–548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2054">39</a></sup>. We also used Augustus (<a href="http://augustus.gobics.de/">http://augustus.gobics.de/</a>), GENSCAN (<a href="http://genes.mit.edu/GENSCAN.html">http://genes.mit.edu/GENSCAN.html</a>), GlimmerHMM (<a href="https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/glimmerhmm/">https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/glimmerhmm/</a>) and SNAP (<a href="http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/software.html">http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/software.html</a>) for <i>ab initio</i> predictions, with model parameters trained using 730 <i>Cephalotus</i> gene models that were well supported by homology evidence. RNA-seq data generated from 16 samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 8</a>) were used for transcript-based predictions with the Bowtie–Tophat–Cufflinks pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2096">40</a></sup>. These models were merged using GLEAN (<a href="http://glean-gene.sourceforge.net/">http://glean-gene.sourceforge.net/</a>). Finally, gene models that were not in the GLEAN non-redundant gene set but supported by both homology and RNA-seq evidences, or homology-based models (frame shift mutation not allowed and aligning rate &gt;50%), or RNA-seq models encoding proteins <span class="stix">≧</span>120 amino acids in length, were further added.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene annotation</h3><p>Gene functions were assigned using BLAST searches (E-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>) against the following databases: KEGG (Release 58), nr (NCBI release 20130904), Swissprot and TrEMBL (Uniprot release 201203). Conserved protein domains were assessed by InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213–D221 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2119">41</a></sup> and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2123">42</a></sup> with applications including HMMPfam, HMMPanther, ProfileScan, HMMSmart, FPrintScan and BlastProDom.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evaluation of genome assembly and gene prediction</h3><p>Gene coverage of predicted gene sets was evaluated using CEGMA 2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2135">43</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1b</a>). Read mapping rates of 15 RNA-seq libraries from five tissues ranged from 74.4% to 83.6% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 9</a>), indicating consistency between the assembled genome and the sequenced transcriptome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Small RNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>Plant tissues were ground in liquid nitrogen using a mortar and pestle. Total RNA was extracted using PureLink Plant RNA Reagent (Life Technologies) and subsequently purified using the miRNeasy kit (QIAGEN). DNase treatment was performed during the column purification. Briefly, for each sample, RNA of the desired size range (18–30 nucleotides) was size-fractionated and ligated with the 5' adapter and, subsequently, the 3' adapter. Ligated RNA was then subjected to PCR with reverse transcription (RT-PCR) to produce sequencing libraries. Small RNA-seq was performed on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">miRNA prediction and target prediction</h3><p><i>Cephalotus</i> miRNA loci were predicted in the genome by both transcriptome- and homology-based methods (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Small RNA-seq reads were mapped onto genomic inverted repeats predicted by EMBOSS einverted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European molecular biology open software suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2169">44</a></sup>. miRNA loci were identified from the mapping results using ShortStack v1.2.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Axtell, M. J. ShortStack: comprehensive annotation and quantification of small RNA genes. RNA 19, 740–751 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2173">45</a></sup>. For homology-based prediction, 7,385 mature miRNA sequences of Viridiplantae species were retrieved from miRbase release 20<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kozomara, A. &amp; Griffiths-Jones, S. miRBase: annotating high confidence microRNAs using deep sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D68–D73 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2177">46</a></sup>. These miRNA sequences were mapped onto the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome using patscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dsouza, M., Larsen, N. &amp; Overbeek, R. Searching for patterns in genomic data. Trends Genet. 13, 497–498 (1997)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2185">47</a></sup>, allowing one mismatch. Putative loci mapped by less than five independent miRNAs were excluded. Secondary structures were identified from flanking regions of mapped loci (±350 bp) using RNAfold of Vienna RNA Package 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lorenz, R. et al. ViennaRNA package 2.0. Algorithms Mol. Biol. 6, 26 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2189">48</a></sup>, and putative miRNA loci were predicted using miRcheck with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jones-Rhoades, M. W. &amp; Bartel, D. P. Computational identification of plant microRNAs and their targets, including a stress-induced miRNA. Mol. Cell 14, 787–799 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2193">49</a></sup>. When putative miRNAs were predicted on both strands of the same loci, the minor locus was collapsed. Putative targets of annotated miRNAs were identified using psRNATarget<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Dai, X. &amp; Zhao, P. X. psRNATarget: a plant small RNA target analysis server. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W155–W159 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2197">50</a></sup> using default settings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">OrthoMCL gene classification</h3><p>Orthologues were clustered by comparison of protein data sets among <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. follicularis</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Coffea canephora</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>U. gibba</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> using BLASTP (cut-off: 10<sup>−5</sup>) and OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2242">6</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 11 and 12</a>). Protein data sets of the nine genomes were BLAST searched against nr (NCBI release 20140407; BLASTP, E-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>). Functional terms (GO and enzyme codes) were then assigned to each query sequence using Blast2GO (<a href="https://www.blast2go.com/">https://www.blast2go.com/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Maximum-likelihood inference of orthogroup gains and losses</h3><p>We estimated the divergence times of the surveyed species using RAxML version 8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2267">51</a></sup>, employing tree topologies published previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2271">52</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2274">54</a></sup>. The reported placement of <i>P. persica</i> (Rosales) is discrepant between plastid-<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2281">54</a></sup> and nuclear-based analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2285">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2288">53</a></sup>. To account for that, we analysed phylogenetic relationships using the single-copy orthologue alignment (see below). Although the bootstrap supports were low, the maximum-likelihood tree supported the nuclear-based topology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1h</a>), and therefore we placed <i>P. persica</i> as sister to the clade containing <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i>. The placement of <i>V. vinifera</i> is also different among previously published phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2315">52</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2318">54</a></sup>. To account for that, two alternative tree topologies with different placements of <i>V. vinifera</i> were assumed in this analysis. For that, we leveraged the amino acid sequence data of all single-copy orthologues, as defined by OrthoMCL (1,836 1:1 orthologues), after excluding all putative TE sequences identified in BLAST searches against different TE databases (TIGR Plant Repeat Databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C. R. The TIGR Plant Repeat Databases: a collective resource for the identification of repetitive sequences in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D360–D363 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2325">55</a></sup>, TransposonPSI (<a href="http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net">http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net</a>) and NCBI's non-redundant (nr) protein database). We then aligned the sequences of each orthogroup with the program M-Coffee<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wallace, I. M., O’Sullivan, O., Higgins, D. G. &amp; Notredame, C. M-Coffee: combining multiple sequence alignment methods with T-Coffee. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, 1692–1699 (2006)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2336">56</a></sup> and used trimAl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Capella-Gutierrez, S., Silla-Martinez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldon, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2341">57</a></sup> to automatically remove poorly aligned regions. The best-fit amino acid substitution model for each multiple sequence alignment was selected using ProtTest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Darriba, D., Taboada, G. L., Doallo, R. &amp; Posada, D. ProtTest 3: fast selection of best-fit models of protein evolution. Bioinformatics 27, 1164–1165 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2345">58</a></sup> and specified in the RAxML analysis under a partitioned scheme. We finally used r8s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Sanderson, M. J. r8s: inferring absolute rates of molecular evolution and divergence times in the absence of a molecular clock. Bioinformatics 19, 301–302 (2003)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2349">59</a></sup> to obtain the ultrametric trees required for the BadiRate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Librado, P., Vieira, F.
