G. &amp; Rozas, J. BadiRate: estimating family turnover rates by likelihood-based methods. Bioinformatics 28, 279–281 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2353">60</a></sup> analysis, by applying the nonparametric rate smoothing algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Sanderson, M. J. r8s: inferring absolute rates of molecular evolution and divergence times in the absence of a molecular clock. Bioinformatics 19, 301–302 (2003)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2357">59</a></sup> to the maximum-likelihood trees and fixing the age of the root to 113 Myr in both cases. This date, a compromise for the two trees we tested, was derived from the average of the 2 BEAST point estimates for the earliest split within the rosid clade (with Vitaceae as one sister lineage), as calculated in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2361">54</a></sup> (their Fig. 1 and Table 2). The two trees tested are detailed below.</p><p>Tree 1:</p><p>((V_vinifera:92.251246,(((T_cacao:72.791098,A_thaliana:72.791098): 5.199433,(P_trichocarpa:72.150935,C_follicularis:72.150935):5.839595):5.054431,P_persica:83.044961):9.206285):20.748754,(U_gibba:101.840606,(C_canephora:89.804910,S_lycopersicum:89.804910):12.035696):11.159394).</p><p>Tree 2:</p><p>(((U_gibba:82.830331,(C_canephora:74.586612,S_lycopersicum:74.586612):8.243718):14.783045,(((T_cacao:74.611384,A_thaliana:74.611384):5.510359,(P_trichocarpa:73.737693,C_follicularis:73.737693):6.384050):5.848981,P_persica:85.970724):11.642652):15.386624,V_vinifera:113.000000).</p><p>To identify gene families specifically expanded in the <i>Cephalotus</i> genome, we followed the method implemented in refs <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2384">4</a></sup> and <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Carretero-Paulet, L. et al. High gene family turnover rates and gene space adaptation in the compact genome of the carnivorous plant Utricularia gibba . Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1284–1295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2388">61</a></sup>, accepting a weighted Akaike information criterion (wAIC) ratio of 2.7 for the best-fit branch model to the second-best-fit model. We ran BadiRate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Librado, P., Vieira, F. G. &amp; Rozas, J. BadiRate: estimating family turnover rates by likelihood-based methods. Bioinformatics 28, 279–281 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2392">60</a></sup> twice, once for each of the two alternative topologies shown above. Only those families strongly supported as expanded (wAIC ratio &gt;2.7) under both of the two alternative topologies were considered for further analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">GO enrichment analysis</h3><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 12</a> shows the per species summary of orthogroups and singletons in nine plant species. Before BadiRate analyses, orthogroups containing sequences with significant similarity to transposable elements (resulting in E-values &lt;10<sup>−15</sup> in TBLASTX searches against sequences of the RepBase v19.12 database)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2412">62</a></sup> were filtered out from all nine genomes. The functional categories (generic GO terms) differentially represented among 493, 495 and 492 <i>Cephalotus</i>-specific expanded genes families (grouping 2,560, 2,567 and 2,557 total genes, respectively), as identified in BadiRate analyses performed using tree 1, tree 2 and the intersection of both trees, are displayed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 15, 16 and 17</a>, respectively. Similarly, differential representation of GO generic terms among 2,716 <i>Cephalotus</i>-specific singletons, 237 <i>Cephalotus</i>-specific two-gene families (474 total genes) and <i>Cephalotus</i>-specific 201 multigene families (1,714 total genes) are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 19, 20 and 21</a>, respectively. Finally, differential representation of GO generic terms among five pairs of genes unique to <i>Cephalotus</i> and <i>U. gibba</i> is presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 18</a>. We performed significance analyses of differential distribution of GO terms by comparing different subsets of genes with the entire complement of genes in the genome using Fisher’s exact test (seefor example, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2445">4</a></sup>). To control for multiple testing, the resulting <i>P</i> values were corrected according to ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2452">63</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Selection of differentially expressed genes</h3><p>Strand-specific RNA-seq reads were mapped to gene models on the genome assembly using Tophat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2464">64</a></sup> with minimum and maximum intron lengths of 20 and 20,000 bp, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Transcript abundances calculated by featureCounts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Liao, Y., Smyth, G. K. &amp; Shi, W. featureCounts: an efficient general purpose program for assigning sequence reads to genomic features. Bioinformatics 30, 923–930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2471">65</a></sup> were normalized using the iterative differentially expressed gene elimination strategy (iDEGES)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Sun, J., Nishiyama, T., Shimizu, K. &amp; Kadota, K. TCC: an R package for comparing tag count data with robust normalization strategies. BMC Bioinformatics 14, 219 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2475">66</a></sup>, which consists of sequential TMM-(edgeR-TMM)<sub><i>n</i></sub> normalization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Robinson, M. D. &amp; Oshlack, A. A scaling normalization method for differential expression analysis of RNA-seq data. Genome Biol. 11, 2010–2011 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2484">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139–140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2487">68</a></sup>. Using the normalized reads per million mapped reads (RPM) values, differentially expressed genes were identified by an exact test for a negative binomial distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Robinson, M. D. &amp; Smyth, G. K. Small-sample estimation of negative binomial dispersion, with applications to SAGE data. Biostatistics 9, 321–332 (2008)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2491">69</a></sup> and subsequent multiple correction by adjusting the false discovery rate to <i>q</i> &lt; 0.01 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2498">63</a></sup>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Fig. 2c,d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Figs 2–5 and 9</a>). Normalized RPM values are used in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Fig. 2c,d</a>, whereas unnormalized RPM values are plotted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Figs 2–5 and 9</a>. The significantly differentially expressed genes were subjected to a subsequent GO-enrichment analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 22 and 23</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Protein sequencing of digestive fluids</h3><p>Digestive fluids of <i>C. follicularis</i>, <i>D. adelae</i>, <i>N. alata</i> and <i>S. purpurea</i> were collected from soil-grown plants in a greenhouse. Fluids were freeze dried and stored at room temperature. Dried samples were dissolved in a protease inhibitor cocktail (cOmplete, Mini, EDTA-free, Roche), precipitated with 8% trichloroacetic acid (TCA) and then washed with 90% acetone. They were dissolved in SDS sample buffer (62.5 mM Tris-HCl, 2% SDS, 0.25% BPB, 10% glycerol, 5% 2-mercaptoethanol, pH 6.8), denatured at 95 °C for 3 min and then separated by 12% SDS-polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis. Negative staining was performed using the Gel-Negative Stain Kit (Nacalai Tesque) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. After destaining, proteins were transferred to polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) membranes. N-terminal sequences of each protein band were determined by the Edman degradation method using an ABI Procise 494-HT instrument (Applied Biosystems). To obtain internal protein sequences, protein bands were dissected from the gel, destained, dehydrated with 100% acetonitrile for 5 min, dried using an evaporator and then reduced by incubating in 10 mM DTT and 25 mM ammonium bicarbonate at 56 °C for 60 min. After washing with 25 mM ammonium bicarbonate, the proteins were alkylated in 55 mM iodoacetamide and 25 mM ammonium bicarbonate for 45 min at room temperature. After washing with 50% acetonitrile containing 25 mM ammonium bicarbonate, the samples were dried using an evaporator. The proteins were in-gel-digested with 10 ng μl<sup>−1</sup> trypsin in 50 mM ammonium bicarbonate, 10 ng μl<sup>−1</sup> lysyl endopeptidase in 25 mM Tris-HCl (pH 9.0) or 20 ng μl<sup>−1</sup> V8 protease in 50 mM phosphate buffer (pH 7.8) at 37 °C overnight. The digested peptides were extracted twice by sonication in 50% acetonitrile containing 5% trifluoroacetic acid (TFA) for 10 min. The peptides were separated by high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) using the Pharmacia SMART System and a reverse-phase column (μRPC C2/C18 PC 3.2/3, GE Healthcare Life Sciences, or XBridge C8 5 μm 2.1×100 mm, Waters) under the following conditions: constant flow rate of 200 μl min<sup>−1</sup>; solvent A, 0.5% TFA, solvent B, acetonitrile containing 0.5% TFA; linear gradient from 10 to 40% (B over A in % (v/v)) over 30 min (1% min<sup>−1</sup>). Separated peptides were then used for protein sequencing by the Edman degradation method.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Transcriptome assembly and identification of transcripts encoding biochemically identified proteins</h3><p>RNA-seq reads of <i>D. adelae</i>, <i>N. alata</i>, and <i>S. purpurea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 8</a>) were assembled into transcripts using Trinity (version r2013-02-25)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2569">70</a></sup> with a 200 bp minimum contig length cut-off. Partial amino acid sequences of digestive fluid proteins were subjected to TBLASTN searches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2574">71</a></sup> against the transcriptome assemblies and the <i>Cephalotus</i> gene models to identify the corresponding transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 25–28</a>). Sequence variants within a Trinity’s component were considered as originating from the same gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Preparation of digestive fluid protein data sets</h3><p>In addition to proteins identified in this study (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 25–28</a>), we obtained for phylogenetic analyses a number of previously published sequences of digestive fluid proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Okabe, T., Yoshimoto, I., Hitoshi, M., Ogawa, T. &amp; Ohyama, T. An S-like ribonuclease gene is used to generate a trap-leaf enzyme in the carnivorous plant Drosera adelae . FEBS Lett. 579, 5729–5733 (2005)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2595">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nishimura, E. et al. S-like ribonuclease gene expression in carnivorous plants. Planta 238, 955–967 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2598">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Paszota, P. et al. Secreted major Venus flytrap chitinase enables digestion of Arthropod prey. Biochim. Biophys. Acta 2, 374–383 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2601">72</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Hatano, N. &amp; Hamada, T. Proteomic analysis of secreted protein induced by a component of prey in pitcher fluid of the carnivorous plant Nepenthes alata . J. Proteomics 75, 4844–4852 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2604">78</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>). Although many protein and transcript sequences for possible digestive enzymes are available (for example, refs <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Schulze, W. X. et al. The protein composition of the digestive fluid from the Venus flytrap sheds light on prey digestion mechanisms. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 11, 1306–1319 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2611">17</a></sup>,<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Renner, T. &amp; Specht, C. D. Molecular and functional evolution of class I chitinases for plant carnivory in the Caryophyllales. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 2971–2985 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2615">79</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Jensen, M. K. et al. Transcriptome and genome size analysis of the Venus flytrap. PLoS ONE 10, e0123887 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2618">84</a></sup>), we included only genes for which complete coding sequences were available and for which their presence in digestive fluid had been biochemically validated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>, last searched 20 January 2016).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Phylogenetic analyses of gene families</h3><p>Phylogenetic relationships of digestive enzyme genes and other carnivory-related genes were analysed along with their homologues in the annotated genomes of ten angiosperm species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 11</a>). TBLASTX searches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2637">71</a></sup> were performed against the above coding sequence (CDS) data sets with an E-value cut-off of 10. After sequence retrieval, multiple alignments were prepared using MAFFT 6.956<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2641">85</a></sup>, and ambiguous codons were removed using trimAl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Capella-Gutierrez, S., Silla-Martinez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldon, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2645">57</a></sup> implemented in Phylogears2-2.0.2013.03.15 (<a href="http://www.fifthdimension.jp/products/phylogears/">http://www.fifthdimension.jp/products/phylogears/</a>) with the ‘gappyout’ option. Poorly aligned sequences were removed using MaxAlign<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Gouveia-Oliveira, R., Sackett, P. W. &amp; Pedersen, A. G. MaxAlign: maximizing usable data in an alignment. BMC Bioinformatics 8, 312 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2657">86</a></sup>. Phylogenetic trees were reconstructed by the maximum-likelihood method using RAxML v8.0.26<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2661">51</a></sup> with the general time-reversible (GTR) model of nucleotide substitution and four discrete gamma categories of rate heterogeneity (‘GTRGAMMA’ option). Support for nodes was estimated by rapid bootstrapping with 100 replicates. Trees were rooted at the midpoint between the two most divergent genes. Gene duplication events shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig2">Figs 2</a>b and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig3">3</a>a were inferred on the basis of species overlap between partitions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Huerta-Cepas, J. et al. The human phylome. Genome Biol. 8, 934–941 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2671">87</a></sup> using a Python package ‘ETE3’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Huerta-Cepas, J., Serra, F. &amp; Bork, P. ETE 3: reconstruction, analysis, and visualization of phylogenomic data. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1635–1638 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2675">88</a></sup>. The trees were visualized using iTOL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life v2: online annotation and display of phylogenetic trees made easy. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W475–W478 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2680">89</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Detection of orthologous relationships</h3><p>Orthology of <i>Cephalotus</i> genes and digestive enzyme genes was inferred on the basis of tree topologies reconstructed by the maximum-likelihood method using the ten plant genomes described above (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Figs 2, 4 and 5</a>). As we cannot exclude the possibility of parallel gene losses, a clade containing genes from at least five plant genomes was designated as a putative orthologous unit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Expression profiling of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes</h3><p>Affymetrix ATH1 (25K) microarray data sets on stress-related experiments were retrieved from ArrayExpress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Kolesnikov, N. et al. ArrayExpress update—simplifying data submissions. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D1113–D1116 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2711">90</a></sup> if two or more replicates were available on wild-type <i>Arabidopsis</i> plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 30</a>). Robust multi-array average normalized expression data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Irizarry, R. A. et al. Exploration, normalization, and summaries of high density oligonucleotide array probe level data. Biostatistics 4, 249–264 (2003)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2721">91</a></sup> were subjected to heatmap visualization using the R package ‘gplots’. Dendrograms were constructed using the furthest neighbour method with Euclidian distances. Significance of differential expression was analysed by a randomization test with 10,000 iterations in which resamplings were performed in each gene family and the sum of expression changes was compared with the original value.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Evaluation of detection methods for molecular convergence</h3><p>To evaluate different tree reconstruction methods, simulated gene sequences were generated using the R package ‘Phylosim’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Sipos, B., Massingham, T., Jordan, G. E. &amp; Goldman, N. PhyloSim - Monte Carlo simulation of sequence evolution in the R statistical computing environment. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 104 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2733">92</a></sup>. We used publicly available simulated data sets for 16 fungi species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Rasmussen, M. D. &amp; Kellis, M. A Bayesian approach for fast and accurate gene tree reconstruction. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 273–290 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2737">93</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Wu, Y. C., Rasmussen, M. D., Bansal, M. S. &amp; Kellis, M. TreeFix: statistically informed gene tree error correction using species trees. Syst. Biol. 62, 110–120 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2740">94</a></sup>. These data sets contain 1,000 simulated tree topologies of gene families, each of which was generated under observed gene duplication and loss rates. Sequences of 300 codons were simulated on the tree topologies of the fungi data set. Codon usage was sampled from the actual frequencies in <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Nakamura, Y., Gojobori, T. &amp; Ikemura, T. Codon usage tabulated from international DNA sequence databases: status for the year 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 292 (2000)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2746">95</a></sup>. The κ (transition/transversion rate) was set to 1. The ω (nonsynonymous / synonymous nucleotide substitution rate ratio (d<i>N</i>/d<i>S</i>)) of each codon position was randomly sampled from a gamma distribution (shape = 0.5, rate = 1). To mimic molecular convergence, two genes were randomly selected to be converged. In terminal branches of selected genes, codon usage of <i>S. cerevisiae</i> was replaced with a biased matrix in which frequencies of codons coding for two randomly selected amino acids were increased. Increased frequency was calculated by multiplying the original value by 100, and then total frequencies of all codons were scaled to 1.</p><p>Gene trees were inferred by the maximum-likelihood method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2763">51</a></sup> using first, second, third and all codon positions as well as 300 nucleotide random sequences. To obtain a robust tree topology, the gene trees were reconciled with the species tree using Treefix 1.1.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Wu, Y. C., Rasmussen, M. D., Bansal, M. S. &amp; Kellis, M. TreeFix: statistically informed gene tree error correction using species trees. Syst. Biol. 62, 110–120 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2767">94</a></sup>, which incorporates duplication-loss parsimony and a test statistic for likelihood equivalence. Reconciliation was accomplished using default settings for which 1,000 iterations of topology searches were performed and rearrangements were accepted when likelihood was not significantly reduced by the Shimodaira–Hasegawa test<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Shimodaira, H. &amp; Hasegawa, M. Multiple comparisons of log-likelihoods with applications to phylogenetic inference. Mol. Biol. Evol. 16, 1114–1116 (1999)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2771">96</a></sup> (<i>P</i> value threshold of 0.05). Branch lengths of reconciled trees were optimized using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2778">51</a></sup>. Finally, the numbers of convergent and divergent substitutions were estimated from the inferred tree topologies and the original simulated alignments using CodeMLancestral<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Castoe, T. A. et al. Evidence for an ancient adaptive episode of convergent molecular evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 8986–8991 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2783">21</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Substitution pairs that result in the same descendant amino acid at the same alignment position in both branches were categorized as convergent changes, whereas the remaining substitution pairs were counted as divergent changes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Castoe, T. A. et al. Evidence for an ancient adaptive episode of convergent molecular evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 8986–8991 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2790">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Goldstein, R. A., Pollard, S. T., Shah, S. D. &amp; Pollock, D. D. Nonadaptive amino acid convergence rates decrease over time. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1373–1381 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2793">28</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Detection of molecular convergence in digestive fluid proteins</h3><p>Genes encoding digestive fluid proteins identified in this study (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 25–28</a>) and previous research (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Table 24</a>) were analysed. When corresponding gene sequences for a given species clustered together in the maximum-likelihood trees (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>), they were considered to represent the same gene, whereafter we retained our own sequences to circumvent incorrect inference of gene duplication events in phylogeny reconciliation. A maximum-likelihood tree was reconstructed using third codon position sequences of the trimAl-processed alignments, and it was subsequently reconciled with a species tree prepared from a dated large-scale plastid phylogeny of flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2814">54</a></sup> using Treefix<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Wu, Y. C., Rasmussen, M. D., Bansal, M. S. &amp; Kellis, M. TreeFix: statistically informed gene tree error correction using species trees. Syst. Biol. 62, 110–120 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2818">94</a></sup> with default parameters, except with the number of iterations increased to 1,000. Although the plastid-based topology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2823">54</a></sup> is partly different from nuclear-based topology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2827">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2830">53</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 1h</a>), we employed it because of the necessity to include carnivorous lineages in which nuclear genome sequences are unavailable (for example, <i>Drosera</i>, <i>Nepenthes</i> and <i>Sarracenia</i>). Branch lengths of the reconciled trees were optimized against trimAl-processed CDS alignments using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2847">51</a></sup>. The trees were subsequently used for Bayesian ancestral state reconstruction using PhyloBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Lartillot, N., Lepage, T. &amp; Blanquart, S. PhyloBayes 3: a Bayesian software package for phylogenetic reconstruction and molecular dating. Bioinformatics 25, 2286–2288 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2851">97</a></sup> over 12,000 generations (2,000 generations of burn-in) with an infinite mixture of GTR substitution models (CAT-GTR model) of amino acid substitution and five discrete gamma categories of rate heterogeneity to calculate posterior numbers of convergent and divergent substitution pairs. Background levels (null hypothesis) of convergent substitution pairs were estimated by a linear regression in which the posterior numbers of convergent changes were predicted by divergent changes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Castoe, T. A. et al. Evidence for an ancient adaptive episode of convergent molecular evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 8986–8991 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2855">21</a></sup>. Over-accumulation of convergent changes in a tree was examined by one-sided single-sample proportion tests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Wilson, E. B. Probable inference, the law of succession, and statistical inference. J. Am. Stat. Assoc. 22, 209–212 (1927)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2859">98</a></sup> with Yate’s continuity correction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Yates, F. Contingency tables involving small numbers and the χ2 test. J. R. Stat. Soc. 1(suppl.) 217–235 (1934)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2863">99</a></sup> and subsequent Bonferroni adjustment for multiple comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Neyman, J. &amp; Pearson, E. S. On the use and interpretation of certain test criteria for purposes of statistical inference: part I. Biometrika 20A, 175–240 (1928)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2867">100</a></sup>. Digestive enzyme branch pairs among independent carnivorous plant lineages were examined in the statistical test. Corrected <i>P</i> values are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 6.</a></p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Homology modelling of protein structures</h3><p>Protein structures of digestive enzymes were analysed using the SWISS-MODEL Workspace<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Biasini, M. et al. SWISS-MODEL: modelling protein tertiary and quaternary structure using evolutionary information. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, W252–W258 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2888">101</a></sup>. Template models were selected using the ‘Template Identification’ tool. SWISS-MODEL Template Library IDs of selected templates were 2dkv.1.A, 3zk4.1.A and 1dix.1.A for GH19 chitinases, purple acid phosphatases and RNase T2s, respectively. Predicted models were visualized using UCSF Chimera 1.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Pettersen, E. F. et al. UCSF Chimera —a visualization system for exploratory research and analysis. J. Comput. Chem. 25, 1605–1612 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2892">102</a></sup>. Relative exposure of amino acid surfaces was calculated by dividing solvent-accessible surface in protein structures by the theoretical maximum of corresponding amino acids in Gly-X-Gly tripeptide contexts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Tien, M. Z., Meyer, A. G., Sydykova, D. K., Spielman, S. J. &amp; Wilke, C. O. Maximum allowed solvent accessibilites of residues in proteins. PLoS ONE 8, e80635 (2013)." href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d41612e2896">103</a></sup>. The relative solvent-accessible surface area for a paired amino acid substitution was reported by averaging values in proteins constituting the two clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM229">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Data availability</h3><p>The <i>Cephalotus</i> genome assembly and gene models are available from the DNA Data Bank of Japan (DDBJ) with the accession numbers <a href="http://getentry.ddbj.nig.ac.jp/getentry/na/BDDD01000001">BDDD01000001</a> to <a href="http://getentry.ddbj.nig.ac.jp/getentry/na/BDDD01016307">BDDD01016307</a>. The genomic sequences, gene models and other source data are also available at CoGe (Genome ID = 29002) and Dryad (doi:<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.50tq3">10.5061/dryad.50tq3</a>). The DDBJ accession numbers for DNA-seq (DRR053706–DRR053720), mRNA-seq (DRR053690–DRR051749; DRR029007–DRR29010) and small RNA-seq (DRR058704–DRR058708) are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 1, 8 and 10</a>, respectively. DDBJ accessions and gene IDs for coding sequences of digestive fluid proteins are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-016-0059#MOESM230">Supplementary Tables 25–28</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec30" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec30-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec30">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec30-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Fukushima, K. <i>et al.</i> Genome of the pitcher plant <i>Cephalotus</i> reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. <i>Nat. Ecol. Evol</i>. <b>1,</b> 0059 (2017).</p></div></div></section>

K.F. and M.H. established culture conditions to control the leaf dimorphism. K.F., X.F., T.N., S.L., and M.H. coordinated genome assembly and annotation. K.F., X.F., C.C., M.N., S.S. and M.X. prepared and sequenced Illumina libraries. M.N. coordinated PacBio sequencing. T.F.S. performed PacBio sequencing. T.I. and M.K. performed error correction of PacBio reads and inter-contig gap filling. K.F., X.F., H.C. and L.M. performed gene prediction and annotation. K.F. performed miRNA prediction and annotation. D.A.-P. performed singleton gene analysis. C.Z. and D.S. performed synteny analysis. P.L., A.S.-G and J.R. performed BadiRate analysis. L.C.-P. and V.A.A. performed GO-enrichment analysis. K.F., K.M.F., T.-H.C. and G.P. performed gene family analysis. T.F., Y. Hiwatashi, Y. Hoshi, H.M., N.S., T.U. and M.H. performed digestive fluid sampling and protein sequencing. K.F., S.T.P. and D.D.P. performed convergence analysis. K.F., V.A.A. and M.H. wrote the paper with input from all authors. M.H. initiated and directed the project. K.F., V.A.A., S.L. and M.H. are representatives of each group. K.F. and X.F. should be considered joint first authors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
